Therefore, we speculate that in Cp0710, a similar mechanism is deployed and the triose phosphate isomerase may be the key enzyme in regulating the carbon flux to fatty acid synthesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>We report in this paper an annotated draft genome of the oleaginous alga Cp0710, a candidate for use as a model alga for biofuel production. Several unique features of the genome were revealed in this analysis. First, the genome size is greatly reduced and the gene number is relatively small, but most of the genes are homologous to <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A, implying that Cp0710 is evolutionarily related but probably distant from other <i>Chlorella</i>. Secondly, the undersized genome of Cp0710 may be due to a smaller number of genes encoded in the genome, fewer copies of duplicated and unique genes, and/or a more ancient genome with fewer duplications and rearrangements. Thirdly, three HUP-like genes, which are unique to <i>Chlorella</i>, were identified in the genome, providing a molecular basis for Cp0710 to be able to consume glucose and grow heterotrophically. Finally, Cp0710 is the only sequenced green alga that lacks both the nitrate and urea transporter, suggesting that it may be unique in nitrogen metabolism. In addition, through comparative transcriptomic and proteomic studies on autotrophic and heterotrophic Cp0710, we generated a global view regarding the changes in metabolic pathways under the two growth conditions. We observed that under heterotrophic conditions, enzymes involved in photosynthesis and CO<sub>2</sub> fixation are almost completely degraded, either as mRNAs or as proteins. Meanwhile, the cells are capable of not only quickly absorbing glucose but also accelerating glucose catabolism through the upregulation of glycolysis and the TCA cycle, which can presumably provide enough energy for both rapid cell growth and enhanced lipid synthesis. Moreover, the rapid synthesis of pyruvate, the upregulation of most enzymes involved in fatty acid synthesis, and the downregulation of enzymes involved in fatty acid degradation favor the accumulation of fatty acids within the cell. This suggests that Cp0710 encodes a fine-tuned pathway for the accumulation of large amounts of lipid under heterotrophic growth conditions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Strain and culture conditions</h3><p>The microalga <i>C. protothecoides</i> strain 0710 was described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Yan D, Dai J, Wu Q: Characterization of an ammonium transporter in the oleaginous alga Chlorella protothecoides. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2013, 97: 919-928." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2164">22</a>]. The medium and culture conditions were the same as described in our previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xiong W, Li X, Xiang J, Wu Q: High-density fermentation of microalga Chlorella protothecoides in bioreactor for microbio-diesel production. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2008, 78: 29-36." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2167">6</a>]. For autotrophic cultivation, the concentration of glycine used was 5 g/L. For heterotrophic cultivation, glucose was added at a concentration of 10 g/L and glycine was reduced to 0.1 g/L.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Harvested cells were ground in liquid nitrogen and the total DNA was isolated using the EZgene TM CP Plant Miniprep Kit (GD2621) following the manufacturer’s protocol. A 300-bp (short-insert) and 2-kb (mate-pair) DNA library were prepared according to standard Illumina DNA preparation protocols and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. A 400–800-bp DNA library was prepared according to the standard Roche 454 DNA preparation protocol and sequenced on the Roche 454 GS FLX platform. Sequencing data from HiSeq 2000 and Roche 454 GS FLX platforms were assembled into contigs using SOAP <i>de novo</i> 2.01 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li R, Zhu H, Ruan J, Qian W, Fang X, Shi Z, Li Y, Li S, Shan G, Kristiansen K, Li S, Yang H, Wang J, Wang J: De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010, 20: 265-272." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2181">37</a>] and Newbler V2.6, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Margulies M, Egholm M, Altman WE, Attiya S, Bader JS, Bemben LA, Berka J, Braverman MS, Chen YJ, Chen Z, Dewell SB, Du L, Fierro JM, Gomes XV, Godwin BC, He W, Helgesen S, Ho CH, Irzyk GP, Jando SC, Alenquer ML, Jarvie TP, Jirage KB, Kim JB, Knight JR, Lanza JR, Leamon JH, Lefkowitz SM, Lei M, Li J, et al: Genome sequencing in microfabricated high-density picolitre reactors. Nature. 2005, 437: 376-380." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2184">38</a>], and further combined with Amos 3.1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Phillippy AM, Schatz MC, Pop M: Genome assembly forensics: finding the elusive mis-assembly. Genome Biol. 2008, 9: R55-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2187">39</a>]. Paired-end information from all reads was used for scaffold building from contigs. Local assembly was applied to fill intra-scaffold gaps with reads from a read pair, in which one read aligned within a contig uniquely and the other located within the gap. Assembly and annotation data have been uploaded with the version number APJO01000000 on DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank (accession APJO00000000). Sequence reads, including both genome and transcriptomes, have been deposited into the NCBI sequence read archive (accessions SRA115225).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome annotation</h3><p>Gene annotation of the Cp0710 genome was accomplished through both a <i>de novo</i>-based approach (Augustus v2.5.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stanke M, Keller O, Gunduz I, Hayes A, Waack S, Morgenstern B: AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006, 34: W435-W439." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2201">40</a>], SNAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Korf I: Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004, 5: 59-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2204">41</a>], and Glimmer HMM v3.02 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL: TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004, 20: 2878-2879." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2207">42</a>]) and a homolog-based approach. The generated gene sets were further combined into a single consensus gene set by applying GLEAN v1.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Elsik CG, Mackey AJ, Reese JT, Milshina NV, Roos DS, Weinstock GM: Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 2007, 8: R13-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2210">43</a>].</p><p>Transcriptome data was also used during the gene annotation of the Cp0710 genome. RNA-seq reads were aligned and assembled into transcripts by Tophat v2.0.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Trapnell C, Pachter L, Salzberg SL: TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2009, 25: 1105-1111." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2216">44</a>] and Cufflinks v2.0.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Trapnell C, Williams BA, Pertea G, Mortazavi A, Kwan G, van Baren MJ, Salzberg SL, Wold BJ, Pachter L: Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat Biotechnol. 2010, 28: 511-515." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2219">45</a>], respectively.</p><p>Gene sets from genome sequencing data and transcriptome sequencing data were further compared to generate one complete set of genes. For those transcriptome-derived genes with complete ORF structure, if a transcriptome-derived gene covered several genome-derived genes, then these genome-derived genes were replaced by the transcriptome-derived gene. Those transcriptome-derived genes without complete ORF structure were further used to help identify the start and stop site of incomplete genes annotated through the homology-based approach.</p><p>Functions were assigned to genes based on the best alignments using Blastp (E-value &lt; = 1e-05) against the Swiss-Prot (release 15.10) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Bairoch A, Boeckmann B, Ferro S, Gasteiger E: Swiss-Prot: juggling between evolution and stability. Brief Bioinform. 2004, 5: 39-55." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2227">46</a>] and KEGG (Release 60.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S: KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000, 28: 27-30." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2230">47</a>] protein databases.</p><p>Applying InterProScan v4.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Phillippy AM, Schatz MC, Pop M: Genome assembly forensics: finding the elusive mis-assembly. Genome Biol. 2008, 9: R55-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2237">39</a>], motifs and domains were annotated by searching against publicly available databases, including ProDom, PRINTS, Pfam, SMART, and PROSITE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hunter S, Jones P, Mitchell A, Apweiler R, Attwood TK, Bateman A, Bernard T, Binns D, Bork P, Burge S, de Castro E, Coggill P, Corbett M, Das U, Daugherty L, Duquenne L, Finn RD, Fraser M, Gough J, Haft D, Hulo N, Kahn D, Kelly E, Letunic I, Lonsdale D, Lopez R, Madera M, Maslen J, McAnulla C, McDowall J, et al: InterPro in 2011: new developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40: D306-D312." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2240">48</a>]<i>.</i> GO (Gene Ontology) descriptions for the individual genes were also obtained from the corresponding information from InterPro.</p><p>The <i>de novo</i> repetitive sequences, including simple repeats, satellites, LINEs, SINEs, and LTR retrotransposons, were identified using LTR_FINDER v1.05 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Xu Z, Wang H: LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W265-W268." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2252">49</a>], PILER v1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Edgar RC, Myers EW: PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21 (Suppl 1): i152-i158." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2255">50</a>], RepeatModeler v1.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>), and RepeatScout v1.0.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA: De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21 (Suppl 1): i351-i358." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2265">51</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Benson G: Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 1999, 27: 573-580." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2269">52</a>]. Known repeats were identified with RepeatMasker v3.2.9 (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org">http://repeatmasker.org</a>) against the Repbase v17.06 database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Jurka J, Kapitonov VV, Pavlicek A, Klonowski P, Kohany O, Walichiewicz J: Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 2005, 110: 462-467." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2279">53</a>]. Additionally, repeat-related proteins were identified using RepeatProteinMask against the protein database in Repbase. The results of the two approaches were combined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene family and phylogenetic tree reconstruction</h3><p>Gene family identification was performed using TreeFam [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li H, Coghlan A, Ruan J, Coin LJ, Hériché JK, Osmotherly L, Li R, Liu T, Zhang Z, Bolund L, Wong GK, Zheng W, Dehal P, Wang J, Durbin R: TreeFam: a curated database of phylogenetic trees of animal gene families. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006, 34: D572-D580." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2290">54</a>]. BLASTP was used to align all protein sequences against the protein dataset of all species with cut-off E-values of 1e-7. For each gene pair with more than 0.33 of the region aligned to both genes, conjoined fragmental alignments were performed using Solar (Sorting Out Local Alignment Results) (a program in TreeFam). The hierarchical clustering algorithm was applied for gene clustering, with thresholds set to a minimum edge weight of ≥10 and a minimum edge density of ≥0.34.</p><p>The protein sequences from 300 single-copy gene families with edge weight &gt;30 and edge density equal to 1, which were conserved among Cp0710, <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A, <i>C. subellipsoidea</i> C-169, <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, <i>V. carteri</i>, <i>M. pusilla</i> CCMP1545, and <i>O. tauri</i>, were extracted and aligned using MUSCLE v3.8.31 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Edgar RC: MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004, 32: 1792-1797." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2315">55</a>]. The multiple alignments were then concatenated into one super sequence for each species. The maximum likelihood tree was constructed with protein sequences using PHYML v3.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Guindon S, Gascuel O: A simple, fast, and accurate algorithm to estimate large phylogenies by maximum likelihood. Syst Biol. 2003, 52: 696-704." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2318">56</a>] under the WAG with gamma model. Bootstrap values were taken to assess branch reliability.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene comparison and synteny analysis</h3><p>The orthologues of Cp0710, <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A, and <i>C. subellipsoidea</i> C-169 were identified using the gene family identification method described above. The orthologues were classified as core and pairwise orthologues. In addition, the core orthologues was divided into 1:1:1 orthologues and n:n:n orthologues. The 1:1:1 orthologues were defined as single-copy genes presenting in all three species and the n:n:n orthologues represented putative multiple gene duplications in at least one of the species. On the other hand, pairwise orthologues were assigned when orthology was not detectable in the third species. Some genes lacking clear orthology, but with some similarity in the other species were designated as “homologous” (E-value cut-off of 10<sup>-5</sup> using BlastP).</p><p>The synteny blocks between Cp0710 and <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A were identified according to the method described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kent WJ, Baertsch R, Hinrichs A, Miller W, Haussler D: Evolution’s cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2003, 100: 11484-11489." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2344">57</a>]. The whole-genome alignments were obtained using the program lastz (Local Alignment Search Tool, blastZ-like, version 1.02.00) in a HOXD55 substitution penalty matrix, and then the aligned gapless segments with the same order and orientation in both species were ligated into “chains” by AXTCHAIN. Subsequently, only a single best alignment for every region of the <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A genome was selected by CHAINNET.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis</h3><p>Total RNAs were isolated from either autotrophic or heterotrophic cells using Trizol Reagent (Invitrogen Inc.), followed by poly (A) mRNA purification with Dynabeads Oligo (dT) (Invitrogen Inc.). The 200-bp cDNA libraries were prepared according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina Inc.). The cDNA libraries derived from enriched mRNA were paired-end sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq™ 2000.</p><p>The unigene expression level was determined using RPKM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Mortazavi A, Williams BA, McCue K, Schaeffer L, Wold B: Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat Methods. 2008, 5: 621-628." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2360">58</a>], and statistical differences in expression of unigenes was determined using SAGE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Audic S, Claverie JM: The significance of digital gene expression profiles. Genome Res. 1997, 7: 986-995." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2363">59</a>]. To rigorously identify differentially expressed genes with multiple pairwise comparisons, false discovery rate (FDR) correction was applied to constrain errors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Benjamini Y, Hochberg Y: Controlling the False Discovery Rate: a Prctical and Powerful Approach to Multiple Testing. J R Stat Soc. 1995, 57: 12-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2366">60</a>]. Finally, unigenes with an FDR &lt; = 0.001 and an RPKM change of &gt; =2-fold were marked as significantly differentially expressed genes (DEGs), between the two samples.</p><p>KEGG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S: KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000, 28: 27-30." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2372">47</a>] pathway enrichment and GO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Ashburner M, Ball CA, Blake JA, Botstein D, Butler H, Cherry JM, Davis AP, Dolinski K, Dwight SS, Eppig JT, Harris MA, Hill DP, Issel-Tarver L, Kasarskis A, Lewis S, Matese JC, Richardson JE, Ringwald M, Rubin GM, Sherlock G: Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat Genet. 2000, 25: 25-29." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2375">61</a>] functional enrichment were then carried out for all DEGs. All upregulated or downregulated genes were initially mapped to KEGG or GO terms in the database (KEGG, release 58; GO, release 2012-07-01) using Blastp, then proteins with E-value ≤ 10<sup>-5</sup> and coverage ≥ 50% were identified. The gene numbers for each term were calculated. Those significantly enriched in GO and KEGG terms were identified by comparison to the genome background using an ultra geometric test with Bonferroni correction, with statistical significance set at P = 0.05. For GO enrichment, to remove redundancy, if the GOs enrich at different levels with parent–child relationship and have the same gene list, the lowest level is chosen.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Protein preparation and identification</h3><p>Cells were collected by centrifugation and resuspended in protein extraction buffer (HEPES-40mM, KCl-10mM, MgCl<sub>2</sub>-10mM, EGTA-5mM, CaCl<sub>2</sub>-10mM, PVPP- 0.1%, PMSF-1mM, and DTT-1mM). Then, the cells were disrupted with a mini-BeadBeater, and the mixture was centrifuged at 15,000 × <i>g</i> for 10 min, and the supernatant was collected. One volume of SDS/sucrose buffer (sucrose 60%, SDS 4%, and 0.1M Tris–HCl, pH 6.8) was added to the supernatant and blended followed by the addition of one volume of Tris-saturated phenol and vortexed for 10 minutes. After centrifugation at 20,000 × <i>g</i> for 10 minutes, the protein-containing layer was collected, and five volumes of a 0.1 M ice cold ammonium acetate–methanol mix was added. The mixture was then incubated at -20°C overnight for protein precipitation. After centrifugation (20,000 × <i>g</i> for 10 min), the pellet was washed three times with 0.1 M ammonium acetate–methanol and twice with acetone. Finally, the protein was lyophilized and dissolved in sample buffer for SDS-PAGE.</p><p>Protein identification was performed as in a previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Zhang N, Zhang Z, Feng S, Wang Q, Malamud D, Deng H: Quantitative analysis of differentially expressed saliva proteins in human immunodeficiency virus type 1 (HIV-1) infected individuals. Anal Chim Acta. 2013, 774: 61-66." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2404">62</a>]. In brief, 100 μg of protein was separated by one dimensional SDS-PAGE. Each lane was cut into 40 slices, and gel digestion was performed for each slice. The digested samples (20 μl) were analyzed by LC-MS/MS with an LTQ Orbitrap Velosmass spectrometer (Thermo Scientific™). Then, 4 μl of each sample was loaded and gradient eluted with a flow rate of 120 nL/min. For the first 2 minutes, the acetonitrile (ACN) content in the wash buffer was 8%, and in the subsequent 43 minutes the ACN content increased to 55%, in the last 15 minutes the ACN content was 95%. The MS analysis was performed in the Orbitrap at 60,000 full width at half-maximum (FWHM) resolutions, and the 20 most abundant ions were selected for the MS/MS analysis performed in the LTQ.</p><p>To identify the proteins, the data from mass spectra were searched against the protein database of Cp0710 using SEQUEST of Thermo Proteome Discoverer 1.2.0.208. The tolerance for peptide identification was 20 ppm in MS and 0.8 Da in MS/MS, and the considered modifications included carbamidomethylation and oxidation. Two missed cleavages were permitted. For quantification, only proteins with PSM ≥ 5 were included in the calculation. The PSM and the total peak area of all peptides for a particular protein were divided by the sum of all PSMs or peak areas of that spectrum, respectively, i.e. normalization to total detected peptides within each sample (central tendency normalization [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Callister SJ, Barry RC, Adkins JN, Johnson ET, Qian WJ, Webb-Robertson BJ, Smith RD, Lipton MS: Normalization approaches for removing systematic biases associated with mass spectrometry and label-free proteomics. J Proteome Res. 2006, 5: 277-286." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d46873e2410">63</a>]) (Sheet Pro with PSM &gt; =5, Column J to Q in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#MOESM2">2</a> Table S10). Then, the protein data were compared between the two samples (Sheet Pro with PSM &gt; =5, Colum R and S in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#MOESM2">2</a> Table S10) to determine the relative change in expression level. This experiment was done twice and only proteins with PSM and area changes ≥ 1.5-fold in both experiments were regarded as significant hits. The raw data from the protein identification is presented in Sheet protein identification in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-15-582#MOESM2">2</a> Table S10. All proteomic data have been submitted to the peptide atlas database (Identifier: PASS00452, <a href="http://www.peptideatlas.org/PASS/PASS00452">http://www.peptideatlas.org/PASS/PASS00452</a>).</p></div></div></section>

YW and YS assembled and annotated the genome, conducted analysis, and wrote part of the paper. DY prepared RNA samples for transcriptome analysis and XH conducted the PFGE. JD and QW conceived the study, conducted analysis, and wrote the paper. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p><p>Chunfang Gao, Yun Wang, Yue Shen contributed equally to this work.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec19" data-title="Electronic supplementary material"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19">Electronic supplementary material</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>
